<div id="review_724325978" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/724325978" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/724325978?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Aubrey by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5746897-ema">Ema</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-translated">reality-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-translated">antidote-translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer724325978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13630432792349056799">I would have rated this higher had not I fallen out of love with sadists a while ago. The vengeful hero, the vigilante serial killer, the commander, the mastermind, the Manhattan Project brain who believed wiping out whole sections of certain hemispheres would bring about world peace. All white, all male, all done to death. Forgive me my lack of impressed engagement upon encountering the latest prototype of trangressive film; for all their displayed atrocities, they refuse to show a woman's body</span>
  <span id="freeText13630432792349056799" style="display:none">I would have rated this higher had not I fallen out of love with sadists a while ago. The vengeful hero, the vigilante serial killer, the commander, the mastermind, the Manhattan Project brain who believed wiping out whole sections of certain hemispheres would bring about world peace. All white, all male, all done to death. Forgive me my lack of impressed engagement upon encountering the latest prototype of trangressive film; for all their displayed atrocities, they refuse to show a woman's body in its natural state of body hair, so really, what exactly are my priorities supposed to be?<blockquote>
  <i>Over his head, lit by the moonlight shining through the gable window, the skeletons of his mother and her baby hang from a beam.</i>
</blockquote>I know this is World War II. I know that this is greatly influenced by the author's own experiences. I know that I, pampered US Millennial that I am, cannot even begin to comprehend what it must have been like to live through such a time. However, I do know fiction, especially when it proclaims itself "objective". I do know what goes into scientific writing, reports and experiments and the distant pall that covers both number and organism. I do know this "objectivity" and its extensions in the writings of government and journalism and everything else that here, in the US, funnels the empathy into the sector of white patriarchy and renders us sadistic to every other tale of horror and woe where the physical characteristics do not fit. I have been moved in the past by such machinations, but not anymore.<blockquote>
  <i>"We killed many people by mistake, but at present everything is being sorted out. We apologize and promise that such mistakes will not be allowed to happen in the future."</i>
</blockquote>The problem is not that I am desensitized to such violence, but that I have been conditioned to see only certain breeds of violence as worthy of not being desensitized to. This realization is what made me take a break from the inundation of WWII fiction, but when I saw the reviews for this book, I was reminded of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6050822.Puhdistus" title="Puhdistus by Sofi Oksanen" rel="nofollow noopener">Purge</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/394535.Blood_Meridian__or_the_Evening_Redness_in_the_West" title="Blood Meridian, or the Evening Redness in the West by Cormac McCarthy" rel="nofollow noopener">Blood Meridian</a> and thought, maybe, this would be different. It was, and yet not, for as per usual all the women were sacrificed for the pain of men, and neither the internalized garroting of Oksanen nor the apocalyptic patriarchy of McCarthy was achieved.<blockquote>
  <i>"I say, "But he won't be here anymore. Order doesn't mean much to me if he isn't here anymore."<br />The old man says "On the contrary. From now on he'll always be with you wherever you go."</i>
</blockquote>The book had its moments, as evidenced by the quotes. However, had I gotten my hands on <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1118616.Le_grand_cahier" title="Le grand cahier by Ágota Kristóf" rel="nofollow noopener">Le grand cahier</a> before the others were translated, I would not have continued.<blockquote>
  <i>"Age doesn't matter. I'm her lover. Is that all you wanted to know?"<br />"No, that's not all. I knew that already. But do you love her?"<br />Lucas opens the door.<br />"I don't know the meaning of that word. No one does. I didn't expect that sort of question from you, Peter."<br />"Nevertheless, you will be asked that sort of question many times in the course of your life. And sometimes you will be obliged to answer it."<br />"And you, Peter? One day you will also be obliged to answer certain questions. I've been to some of your political meetings. You make speeches, the audience applauds. Do you really believe in what you say?"<br />"I have to believe it."<br />"But in your deepest self, what do you think?"<br />"I don't think. I can't allow myself the luxury. I've lived with fear since I was a child.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="13630432792349056799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to M. by: </span>
      Marial, Garima, and Dolors
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5562041-m-sarki?shelf=5-star-wonders">5-star-wonders</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer819375379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11177352920959334464"><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/74935375533/agota-kristof-and-her-trilogy'>http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/7493537...</a><br/><br/><i>He says to me, "We're all dying of one thing or another. That's what all the experts say, anyway."<br/>"What else do they say, the experts?"<br/>"That the world is fucked. And that there's nothing to do about it. It's too late."</i><br/><br/>My wife and I have an English Golden Retriever, a cream-colored animal, a thoroughbred of the dog genus, handsome, smart, dignified, with nary a mean bone in his body. Still a pup, he loves to play and wrestle hard, but at two-and-a-half year</span>
  <span id="freeText11177352920959334464" style="display:none"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/74935375533/agota-kristof-and-her-trilogy">http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/7493537...</a><br /><br /><i>He says to me, "We're all dying of one thing or another. That's what all the experts say, anyway."<br />"What else do they say, the experts?"<br />"That the world is fucked. And that there's nothing to do about it. It's too late."</i><br /><br />My wife and I have an English Golden Retriever, a cream-colored animal, a thoroughbred of the dog genus, handsome, smart, dignified, with nary a mean bone in his body. Still a pup, he loves to play and wrestle hard, but at two-and-a-half years old now he is gradually maturing. But he isn't the typical humanized domestic pet. He moseys around the house to whatever room we might be in in order to check-in once and a while, but rarely does he hang out with the two of us, that is, my wife and I, we being leftovers and the extent of our family at home these days. We believe we are witnessing again, in doggy world lingo, an <i>only-child syndrome</i>. A situation in which there is perhaps too much togetherness within our present family of three, and the dog-child ends up needing more space and sense of its own separateness, as in protecting his precious autonomy. My wife, at times, thinks perhaps Bob doesn't like us, or is pouting, or even as our own human son for a time exhibited, an awful, erratic teenage attitude. But I think Bob the dog is simply letting us know he is an animal, that he likes being an animal, and though he appreciates being fed and looked after by us two-legged people he has no desire to be moulded into some anthropomorphic version of a lap dog we see other dog owners seemingly so proud of and comfortable with. Golden Retrievers are known to be people-dogs, loving and tender, a true friend for their entire life span. Bob is one cool character, though a bit aloof, and his adoptive parents are too, in the sense we are friendly enough to acquaintances but have no great desire to be best friends and hang out together. Our dog, as they say, has taken on the personality of his owners. And that anecdote related above is basically how I felt while reading this wonderful trilogy written by Ágota Kristof. <br /><br />To be more forthcoming I would describe the novel as being brief sentences erupting from a fragmented mind. It is a trilogy that easily takes one in, seamlessly connects the reader emotionally, and generates a momentum and desire to read through to the end. Great story lines, but still none you can really count on. There are lies aplenty here, with fictions enough to get us everywhere we need to go. But it isn't always where we want to be. I much rather preferred the first book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19921345.The_Notebook" title="The Notebook by Ágota Kristof" rel="nofollow noopener">The Notebook</a>, to the last two. However, I did enjoy very much the second book titled <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/230525.The_Proof" title="The Proof by Ágota Kristof" rel="nofollow noopener">The Proof</a>. By the time I got to book three it was both understandable and disconcerting to me for it to be called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/230529.The_Third_Lie" title="The Third Lie by Ágota Kristof" rel="nofollow noopener">The Third Lie</a>. But what is a serious and addictive reader to do? <br /><br />There is really nothing for me to say to expound on anything gifted others have already said about this trilogy. For me, there was something very enjoyable in reading it and also it was discomfiting in a perverted sort of way. I absolutely loved the sex scenes, and they seemed to be little enough, though they were placed just where they needed to be. I also think it helped that this particular woman wrote them. I loved that, and she made me wish for more indiscretions involving her characters. But that is not at all what the books were about. If a reader is looking for an aggressive war novel there is plenty of that going on, but the story comes at you as collateral damages instead of gallant, patriotic victories we seem to be so inundated with today. <br /><br />There is a distance to cross in this master work. But there is also a gap in it that cannot be bridged. The book is most certainly a tender love story that remains for me aloof. Just as Bob the dog is the most loving animal on the planet he steadfastly protects his sacred space. I have to believe <i>The Notebook Trilogy</i> does so too. <br /><br /><i>I go to bed and before falling asleep I talk to Lucas in my head the way I have for many years. What I tell him is just about what I usually do. I tell him if he's dead he's lucky and I'd very much like to be in his place. I tell him he got the better deal, that it is I who is pulling his greater weight. I tell him that life is totally useless, that it's nonsense, an aberration, infinite suffering, the invention of a non-God whose evil surpasses understanding.<i> </i></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11177352920959334464" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_80102048" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80102048" />
    <a title="Lee Klein " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein"><img alt="Lee Klein " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594811741p2/90786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/80102048?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Klein " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Klein " href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein">Lee Klein </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer80102048" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15420847929903034288">The first part ("The Notebook") was wonderfully effed-up reading: first-person plural ("we") narrator and short chapters, studded with sensationalist psychoerotic gruesomeness, focused on supersmart sinister twins during wartime. Spareness plus unspecified time/space coordinates gave it a mythic/fabulist vibe. Two-hundred pages of controlled horrifics, albeit with maybe a bit of a guilty sense for me that this wasn't quite "Waiting for the Barbarians," "Blindness," or "In the Penal Colony" -- ma</span>
  <span id="freeText15420847929903034288" style="display:none">The first part ("The Notebook") was wonderfully effed-up reading: first-person plural ("we") narrator and short chapters, studded with sensationalist psychoerotic gruesomeness, focused on supersmart sinister twins during wartime. Spareness plus unspecified time/space coordinates gave it a mythic/fabulist vibe. Two-hundred pages of controlled horrifics, albeit with maybe a bit of a guilty sense for me that this wasn't quite "Waiting for the Barbarians," "Blindness," or "In the Penal Colony" -- maybe I was more into it for the psychoerotic insanities than stuff about how folks endure the genocidal atrocities, enemy occupations, etc, that warp them? <br /><br />The second and third sections fell way off from the first section, mostly I think because the awesome first-person plural point of view in "The Notebook" shifts to third person ("The Proof"), then first person ("The Third Lie"). Also, it turns out I must've really been swayed by the psychoerotic nastinesses in the first book because their lack in the other two I very much noted. I skimmed the last book, admittedly -- not engaged by the writing (mainly) or the unreliable nature of storytelling subtextual schtuff. But still, the first book was 4.75 stars for me (at least) and some of the second one was not so bad either (3 stars?). <br /><br />Something else: it's clear that the author also writes for the theatre. The first two parts are so spare they're practically scripts. Very little exposition, mostly dialogue, a few stage directions. Works perfectly for the very young, sympathetic, batshit narrator(s).<br /><br />(The <a href="http://www.theonion.com/content/video/denmark_introduces_harrowing_new" rel="nofollow noopener">Lars Von Trier</a> adaptation is gonna rock.)<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15420847929903034288" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_135842695" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/135842695" />
    <a title="Jimmy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1325473-jimmy"><img alt="Jimmy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1364565813p2/1325473.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/135842695?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jimmy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jimmy" href="/user/show/1325473-jimmy">Jimmy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1325473-jimmy?shelf=year-1980s">year-1980s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1325473-jimmy?shelf=female">female</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1325473-jimmy?shelf=and-a-half-stars">and-a-half-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1325473-jimmy?shelf=hungary">hungary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer135842695" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11162956820587488716"><b>Exercise in Leaving Things Out</b><br/><br/>Whatever you do, do not tell the whole story. Leave out entire scenes, only to be hinted at later in a thrown off line. Leave out the feelings. Especially love, don't mention love at all. Describe the cruelties but do not say why they were performed. Tell the story, but leave almost everything out so that it can grow wild like an untended garden in the reader's mind. <br/><br/><b>Exercise in Shifting Perspectives</b><br/><br/>Tell the story together, conjoined as one. Then tell the story apar</span>
  <span id="freeText11162956820587488716" style="display:none"><b>Exercise in Leaving Things Out</b><br /><br />Whatever you do, do not tell the whole story. Leave out entire scenes, only to be hinted at later in a thrown off line. Leave out the feelings. Especially love, don't mention love at all. Describe the cruelties but do not say why they were performed. Tell the story, but leave almost everything out so that it can grow wild like an untended garden in the reader's mind. <br /><br /><b>Exercise in Shifting Perspectives</b><br /><br />Tell the story together, conjoined as one. Then tell the story apart. Tell the story in the third person. The third person tells the third lie. Tell the story in the first person, but then move outwards from that person as if you're in the midst of an out of body experience. Call that person simply 'the child'.<br /><br /><b>Exercise in Lying</b><br /><br />Lie all the truth, but lie it slant. A lie can sometimes start for no reason, like a wildfire. Other times, it is to protect someone you love, but remember don't mention the word love. Other times, a lie can protect something, a manuscript scrawled in a notebook from being destroyed by the officials. Still other times, a lie is preferred because the truth is too painful. Names are hidden like children under the baseboards. Lie about your past, about your family, about where you were all night. But most of all, lie so that the truth comes out stronger.<br /><br /><b>Exercise in Writing a Book Review</b><br /><br />I loved this book so much. Sorry, I wasn't supposed to say that word. Her sentences are spare, pared down to just enough to convey, but then she leaves in a little bit of detail in, just the right ones to paint the entire scene. In this, it reminded me of some of Emmanuel Bove's sentences, though his tone is very different. Much of the story is conveyed in bits and pieces, you find out later in their dialogue what happens, but you can only imply what they felt when that happened. This makes it all the more powerful. <br /><br />I'm still not sure what I think of the third part of the trilogy. (SPOILERS ahead:) The third lie is the third book, meaning the first two books are lies too. I want to be a better reader, I want to be able to take the metafictional element in stride, but maybe because the first two parts were so powerful, it hurts me to think of them as lies. Although different versions of the same truth. Then again, I am reading fiction, i.e. lies. But oh how I hate to be reminded!<br /><br />Or at least, it feels like she is doing something completely different in the third part, that I also admire, but wish it wasn't connected to the first two parts. Something akin to <i>If On a Winters Night a Traveler</i> in a book that's already established itself as something else. But I still love the third part, just as something completely different. I would give the first book 5 stars, the second 4.5 stars and the third 4 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11162956820587488716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Ray" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4908095-ray"><img alt="Ray" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1296856105p2/4908095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1856759437?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ray" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ray" href="/user/show/4908095-ray">Ray</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1856759437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17162303645966707425">A linked series of novellas, which tells the tale of two boys in WW2 eastern Europe. The country is not spelt out but I assume it is Hungary. A fascinating book.<br/><br/>It is almost impossible to do this book justice in a review, but here goes.<br/><br/>Claus and Lucas are twins. They are left by their mother with their grandmother. They grow up in their own world, almost oblivious to the chaos of war all around them. They develop their own rules of conduct and are utterly immoral. Theft, blackmail and murder are</span>
  <span id="freeText17162303645966707425" style="display:none">A linked series of novellas, which tells the tale of two boys in WW2 eastern Europe. The country is not spelt out but I assume it is Hungary. A fascinating book.<br /><br />It is almost impossible to do this book justice in a review, but here goes.<br /><br />Claus and Lucas are twins. They are left by their mother with their grandmother. They grow up in their own world, almost oblivious to the chaos of war all around them. They develop their own rules of conduct and are utterly immoral. Theft, blackmail and murder are nothing to them.<br /><br />When the Soviets drive out the Nazis one of the boys escapes to the West, leaving the other behind - who forms his own little family and manages to create a life for himself in the socialist utopia. It is difficult at times as he has very little paperwork to prove his existance<br /><br />In the final novella the other twin comes back from the West. But his memories are all screwed up - and where is his twin? Did he even exist?<br /><br />These books play with what is truth and what is memory in a way that is hard to portray but utterly compelling and effective. There are shades of The Tin Drum in the way that you are never quite sure what has happened (these two and Oscar Matzerath in a play group would be a sight to behold, best have an ambulance on standby too)<br /><br />Do read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17162303645966707425" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3450968147" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Em*bedded-in-books*" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2481560-em-bedded-in-books"><img alt="Em*bedded-in-books*" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605433673p2/2481560.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3450968147?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Em*bedded-in-books*" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Em*bedded-in-books*" href="/user/show/2481560-em-bedded-in-books">Em*bedded-in-books*</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer10986862656062316480">This one contains one short novel that should be by all means be a "new classic" of our times - ok the late 1980's when it was first published. This is The Notebook which is just awesome, mind blowing and something utterly original - very dark, graphic and explicit so not for everyone but awesome nonetheless<br/><br/>The Proof that directly continues The Notebook and The Third Lie that reinterprets all that came before are excellent too, but they are a bit superfluous and The Notebook should have been lef</span>
  <span id="freeText10986862656062316480" style="display:none">This one contains one short novel that should be by all means be a "new classic" of our times - ok the late 1980's when it was first published. This is The Notebook which is just awesome, mind blowing and something utterly original - very dark, graphic and explicit so not for everyone but awesome nonetheless<br /><br />The Proof that directly continues The Notebook and The Third Lie that reinterprets all that came before are excellent too, but they are a bit superfluous and The Notebook should have been left on its own. The other two are much more conventional with stuff we have seen before - still dark and occasionally explicit - and they cannot help but lessen the impact of the Notebook.<br /><br />The stark, short sentences, short chapters structure of The Notebook as it befits its children POV(s) is just un-replicable in a more conventional tale like the next two.<br /><br />Here we have a case where the one part is much superior to the whole despite that the other two parts would have been great without it too...<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10986862656062316480" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1774003753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1774003753" />
    <a title="Tiffany Miss.Fiction" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47408323-tiffany-miss-fiction"><img alt="Tiffany Miss.Fiction" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459535810p2/47408323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1774003753?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tiffany Miss.Fiction" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tiffany Miss.Fiction" href="/user/show/47408323-tiffany-miss-fiction">Tiffany Miss.Fiction</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1774003753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3603091444797691282">This is not a book. This is a lifetime experience. A travel into your doppelganger and inside your most intimate fears and weakness. I loved and hated every minute of it, it made me cry and made me feel uneasy all the time. I think this is what makes a great book. It doesn't have to be pretty and predictable, it should preferably cut your throat when it finds you unguarded and make you feel uncomfortable, question your safe areas and give you no rest. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_771162493" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/771162493" />
    <a title="Ruby  Tombstone [With A Vengeance]" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5973086-ruby-tombstone-with-a-vengeance"><img alt="Ruby  Tombstone [With A Vengeance]" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444744872p2/5973086.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/771162493?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ruby  Tombstone [With A Vengeance]" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ruby  Tombstone [With A Vengeance]" href="/user/show/5973086-ruby-tombstone-with-a-vengeance">Ruby  Tombstone [With A Vengeance]</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5973086-ruby-tombstone-with-a-vengeance?shelf=literally-hungarian">literally-hungarian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5973086-ruby-tombstone-with-a-vengeance?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5973086-ruby-tombstone-with-a-vengeance?shelf=an-ill-wind-blows-no-minds">an-ill-wind-blows-no-minds</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5973086-ruby-tombstone-with-a-vengeance?shelf=meta">meta</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5973086-ruby-tombstone-with-a-vengeance?shelf=war-war-war-what-is-it-good-for">war-war-war-what-is-it-good-for</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer771162493" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15282142546324293759">1)This book tells the story of Hungarian twin brothers, Lucas & Claus growing up together in the house of their grandmother during WWII.<br/>2)This book is heavy-going, with a challenging writing style.<br/>3)This book is a multi-layered study of the effects of grief, isolation and unbearable loss upon the mind and memory.<br/>4)This book shows how metafiction can be used to best effect in demonstrating how memory, reality, truth and identity are all completely subjective and mutable.<br/>5)This book is bleak from </span>
  <span id="freeText15282142546324293759" style="display:none">1)This book tells the story of Hungarian twin brothers, Lucas &amp; Claus growing up together in the house of their grandmother during WWII.<br />2)This book is heavy-going, with a challenging writing style.<br />3)This book is a multi-layered study of the effects of grief, isolation and unbearable loss upon the mind and memory.<br />4)This book shows how metafiction can be used to best effect in demonstrating how memory, reality, truth and identity are all completely subjective and mutable.<br />5)This book is bleak from start to finish.<br />6)This book is one of the most deeply affecting books I've ever read.<br /><b>
  <u>These are my notes, in which there are three lies.</u>
</b><br /><br /><b>The Lies</b><br />1) The book does tell the story of twins, but the big question throughout the three novels is what really happened to these two young boys growing up. Whatever it was, it was terrible. Their country (never named) was continually occupied by one force or another, times were tough, loss and grief pervaded their world. There was separation, hardship and need, but the boys were adaptive. They did what they needed to in order to survive - including reinventing themselves, each other and those around them. The book is as much the story of the human brain and how it copes with trauma as anything else.<br /><br />2) This book is not at ALL heavy-going. The style of writing is sparse, pared back to the bare essentials. It flies by far too quickly. The first novel is written in the form of short compositions, as in the notebook the children keep. As they explain it, "<i>
  <b>..we have a very simple rule: the composition must be true. We must describe what is, what we see, what we hear, what we do. For example, it is forbidden to write, "Grandmother is like a witch"; but we are allowed to write, "People call Grandmother the Witch."</b>
</i> The other two novels are similarly styled, more descriptive, but still very spare.<br /><br />5) This book is not ALL bleak. True, there are more than enough heartbreaking stories in it to last a lifetime. Take this, for example:<br />"<i>
  <b>..I answer that I try to write true stories but that at a given point the story becomes unbearable because of it’s very truth, and then I have to change it. I tell her that I try to tell my story but all of a sudden I can’t-I don’t have the courage, it hurts too much. And so I embellish everything and describe things not as they happened but the way I wished they happened.<br />She says, “Yes, there are lives sadder than the saddest of books.” I say, “Yes. No book, no matter how sad, can be as sad as a life.”</b>
</i><br />As a whole, the book is devastating. But there is also a black humour that runs through it, particularly the first novel, and scenes that are quite beautiful and compassionate. I truly did not want this book to end. <br /><br />This is as an absolutely astonishing piece of literature. I wish I could say more about it without giving away too much. All I CAN say is that if you're thinking of giving Hungarian literature a go, this is an excellent place to start.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15282142546324293759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1146035662" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1146035662" />
    <a title="bobbygw  " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2692376-bobbygw"><img alt="bobbygw  " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1251838122p2/2692376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1146035662?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="bobbygw  " class="user" itemprop="url" name="bobbygw  " href="/user/show/2692376-bobbygw">bobbygw  </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2692376-bobbygw?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2692376-bobbygw?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2692376-bobbygw?shelf=war">war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1146035662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13376642050948418883">Though published as a trilogy, this is effectively one novel, fragmented into three parts, written by an unreliable narrator. The first part, which won two prestigious European literary awards and was Kristof's debut novel, published when the author was 51, is so utterly astonishing and brilliantly written - in the voice of a moral fable and using beautifully simple, compelling language - that it drives the whole narrative of a troubling conflict both psychological and circumstantial.<br/><br/>It's writte</span>
  <span id="freeText13376642050948418883" style="display:none">Though published as a trilogy, this is effectively one novel, fragmented into three parts, written by an unreliable narrator. The first part, which won two prestigious European literary awards and was Kristof's debut novel, published when the author was 51, is so utterly astonishing and brilliantly written - in the voice of a moral fable and using beautifully simple, compelling language - that it drives the whole narrative of a troubling conflict both psychological and circumstantial.<br /><br />It's written, as they all are, from a first person narrator viewpoint. What's remarkable is the pure clarity of voice and language and - most importantly - the absence of judgement and conventional, socially acceptable behaviour.<br /><br />With the onslaught of WW2, in an unnamed Hungary, identical twin brothers C(K)laus and Lucas record an objective narrative of each day or days of significance that each agrees must be dependent solely on fact. Any emotional, moral or adjectival judgement or even sentiment in their respective stories is eliminated by the brothers.<br /><br />Only once both approve of each other's handwritten version (the day's events being transcribed by each on a daily basis and every script is swapped between the brothers to be evaluated by each in turn), the best one (ie objective, without judgement) is then transcribed and only then written up in The Notebook.<br /><br />Their purity of purpose and determination to lead lives grounded in their own way, extends to them deciding to strip themselves of emotion as they learn to disregard and be invulnerable to the devastating consequences of war. They also choose to act on occasion in ways that threaten and harm those who harm others, and always based upon their own distinctive, yet always logical, moral code. As a metaphor for the impact of war on children it's a powerful and compelling one.<br /><br />The more you read, the more troubling the story, yet the more enriched and nuanced the story becomes.<br /><br />The trilogy resonates in a framework of war and its concomitant troubles and horrors. Limbs and people destroyed, antisemitism wreaked, loved ones blown apart. Life goes on, but always the factual narrative strips life of value and meaning. Yet the brothers survive, though it's clear they're not in any meaningful way living. There's no joy felt or shared, and the tone becomes increasingly despairing and melancholic, as WW2 transitions to what is clearly meant to be Russia's occupation of the country.<br /><br />To describe the plots would be to undermine the intensity of loss, sadness, disorientation and trauma of war inflicted upon the narrator(s). What matters most is the extraordinary perspective of the brothers/unreliable narrator.<br /><br />It's a troubling, powerful, moving story, driven by conflict, often disturbed and disorientating. It never resolves, which, given the subject matter of loss, is surely a morally honest conclusion and one free from false sentiment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13376642050948418883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1978320671" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1978320671" />
    <a title="Krubha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8913754-krubha"><img alt="Krubha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582558239p2/8913754.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1978320671?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krubha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krubha" href="/user/show/8913754-krubha">Krubha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8913754-krubha?shelf=reco-whatsapp">reco-whatsapp</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8913754-krubha?shelf=2020reads">2020reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8913754-krubha?shelf=chgwit2020">chgwit2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8913754-krubha?shelf=chgwoman2020">chgwoman2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8913754-krubha?shelf=spinecrackers">spinecrackers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8913754-krubha?shelf=chgarmchair2020ir">chgarmchair2020ir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1978320671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16692454318259999312"><b>
  <u>The Notebook -</u>
</b>narrated from the perspective of the Twins (not named till end of book 1) in first person plural - was shocking. The exercises they subject themselves to, sounded harsh, but considering the war and poverty of the times, could be taken as crude reality presented raw. The name of the places, and the details of the war, who were the foreigners go unmentioned, with little demarcation on the historical background of the story and the associated facts. But then war is horrible and so</span>
  <span id="freeText16692454318259999312" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>The Notebook -</u>
</b>narrated from the perspective of the Twins (not named till end of book 1) in first person plural - was shocking. The exercises they subject themselves to, sounded harsh, but considering the war and poverty of the times, could be taken as crude reality presented raw. The name of the places, and the details of the war, who were the foreigners go unmentioned, with little demarcation on the historical background of the story and the associated facts. But then war is horrible and so are all that it brings upon innocents. <br /><br />Enter the girl with the hare-lip and the story takes off on a different trajectory. Her part of the story is gruesome but more so with all those graphic details that don't seem necessary, considering the age of the kids narrating. After revolving around several characters be it the foreign officer or the priest or the housekeeper, the story comes back to the Twins, their grandmother and their mother. <br /><br />A few questions are left unanswered as Book 1 ends. Around 2.5 - 2.8 stars, as the entire book kept me wondering whether I was reading the same book as everyone else did.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>The Proof-</u>
</b> started better, with the Twins getting a name. There are many characters that make an entry, and create multiple branches to the main story. But book 1 prepared you for this, and this is still better than book 1, especially Mathias, and his relationship with Lucas. Deserved 3 stars.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>The Third Lie-</u>
</b> 3 the finale, started with a declaration that everything in books 1 and 2 were lies (in fact book 2 ended as a precursor to this revelation). I have read books with unreliable narrator, what keeps them interesting is the twist to all that the reader believed so far, and the actual reasons behind the narrator's twisting of them. Does this unreliability add value to this plot? I don't think so, considering these twists really don't make it intriguing. <br /><br />Book 3 has two parts, first declaration of everything in two books as lie, followed by part 2 that twists part 1 itself, and the story was moving all zigzag to such an extent I hardly cared, be they Twins with no names, or with homonyms for names, or siblings or whatever. After 92% it was just pure perseverance and my OCD, that kept me going to complete this.<br /><br />To summarize in her own words, -<i>I try to write true stories but that at a given point the story becomes unbearable because of its very truth, and then I have to change it. <s>I tell her that</s> I try to tell my story but all of a sudden I can't—I don't have the courage, it hurts too much. And so I embellish everything and describe things not as they happened but the way I wish they had happened.</i><br /><br />Highly disappointing to say the least for putting up with all the biting raw and crude details.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16692454318259999312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2713897696" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2713897696" />
    <a title="Bjorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn"><img alt="Bjorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367928200p2/19667916.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2713897696?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bjorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bjorn" href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn">Bjorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789146235217" href="/book/show/43173195-den-stora-skrivboken-beviset-den-tredje-l-gnen">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19667916-bjorn?shelf=switzerland">switzerland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19667916-bjorn?shelf=hungary">hungary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2713897696" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2834488520583282541">A staggering literary kaleidoscope - the same story three times, except shaken up between every telling to extend the timeframe (war, post-war, post-wall) and change all the details. Heartbreaking, yet a complete joy to read, a language that remains crystal clear and enthralling even as we're led deeper into a labyrinth of unspeakable (and therefore changed) details.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_724713423" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/724713423" />
    <a title="Xandra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6600821-xandra"><img alt="Xandra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587055091p2/6600821.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/724713423?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Xandra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Xandra" href="/user/show/6600821-xandra">Xandra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=some-lgbt-themes">some-lgbt-themes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=by-women">by-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=european">european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=1950-1999">1950-1999</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6600821-xandra?shelf=hungarian">hungarian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer724713423" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15969386465550169783">The twins arrive at their Grandmother’s house in the Little Town carrying their little suitcases. Until Mother comes back, to get used to abuse, disappointment, pain, hunger, and life’s other vicissitudes during the war, they come up with necessary exercises.<br/><br/>Grandmother often hits them. Other people do too. They slap and kick them without reason. The blows hurt and make them cry. To toughen their bodies so they can bear pain without crying, the twins start their first exercise.<br/><br/><blockquote>We are naked. We h</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText15969386465550169783" style="display:none">The twins arrive at their Grandmother’s house in the Little Town carrying their little suitcases. Until Mother comes back, to get used to abuse, disappointment, pain, hunger, and life’s other vicissitudes during the war, they come up with necessary exercises.<br /><br />Grandmother often hits them. Other people do too. They slap and kick them without reason. The blows hurt and make them cry. To toughen their bodies so they can bear pain without crying, the twins start their first exercise.<br /><br /><blockquote>We are naked. We hit one another with a belt. At each blow we say:<br />"It doesn't hurt."<br />We hit harder, harder and harder.<br />We put our hands over a flame. We cut our thighs, our arms, our chests with a knife and pour alcohol on our wounds. Each time we say:<br />"It doesn't hurt."<br />After a while, we really don't feel anything anymore.</blockquote><br />People humiliate them with hurtful insults: "Sons of a witch! Sons of a whore!" Others say: "Idiots! Hoodlums! Snot-nosed kids! Asses! Slobs! Pigs! Devils! Bastards! Little shits! Punks!” To overcome the shame and pain the words carry, they insult each other. <br /><br /><blockquote>We sit down at the kitchen table face to face, and looking each other in the eyes, we say more and more terrible words.<br />One of us says:<br />"Turd! Asshole!"<br />The other one says:<br />"Faggot! Prick!"<br />We go on like that until the words no longer reach our brains, no longer even reach our ears.</blockquote><br />They beg in order to observe people’s reactions, they pretend to be blind and deaf, they learn to bear hunger by starving themselves. As war advances, the exercises become progressively worse. They blackmail, they deceive, they torture animals… <br /><br /><blockquote>One day we hang our cat, a ginger tom, from the branch of a tree. As he hangs, he stretches and grows enormous. He has spasms and convulsions. When he isn't moving anymore, we cut him down. He lies sprawled on the grass, motionless, then suddenly gets up and runs off. Ever since then, we sometimes see him at a distance, but he no longer comes near the house. He doesn't even come to drink the milk we put in front of the door on a little plate.<br />Grandmother says:<br />"That cat is getting wilder and wilder."<br />We say:<br />"Don't worry, Grandmother, we'll take care of the mice."<br />We make traps and drown the mice we catch in boiling water.</blockquote><br />…they kill.<br /><br /><blockquote>"Just set the fire, if you're capable of it."<br />"Yes, madam, we are capable of it. You can depend on us."<br />We slit her throat with a stroke of the razor, then we go and siphon some gasoline from an army vehicle. We pour the gasoline over both bodies and on the walls of the shack. We set fire to it and go home.</blockquote><br />Nothing is more chilling, however, than the following two lines. I will not provide any context, surely you’ll agree after you read the book.<br /><br /><blockquote>"Did something happen?" We say:<br />"Yes, a shell made a hole in the garden."</blockquote><br />The first in the trilogy, <b>The Notebook</b> is what I always imagined war must feel like. An augmentation of our ugly traits, an exaggeration of our dormant cruelty, free of sentimentality and morality, free of humanity even, destructive, glacial and oppressive, when compassion can equate murder and death is part of the daily routine. The twins in <i>The Notebook</i> must be one of the best representations of war ever created.<br /><br />Indicative of an intelligent writer, each of the many short chapters ends on a powerful note. Sad, tragic, ironic, always clever. Knowing when and how to stop, yet not stopping at all because the words linger on, that’s a trait of an excellent novelist.<br /><br /><b>The Proof</b> is outstanding too and <b>The Third Lie</b>, although not as intense as its predecessors, doesn’t disappoint either. And they’re sadder, if that’s even possible. We see how life doesn’t need a war to be full of misery and the perception of these books is shaping accordingly after every revelation, after every lie, until the implacable, tragic conclusion.<br /><br />There are many wonderful, heartfelt reviews for this book. I only tried to convey a feel of it and the quotes I included, shocking and unsentimental, are just preparatory for a much vaster display of horror.<br /><br />[<i>The Notebook</i> was adapted into a <a href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt2324384/" rel="nofollow noopener">movie</a>]</span>
  <a data-text-id="15969386465550169783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5J+JndYWjMm9wuVEWooE2nr+gIY4HQiX/9oykmclsJUYt0ERJh3kvW9aDIvDoCjz/WimyuTV8Ed8c9EpPQpMTA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ZqOJRInBSswMuHufKiy8/NHP7A2F/MkDCF/kPyF/H7Sai0HIecoiuN4gklCzBpDVVlnKQVk0MdOL9geEe1DjbQ==')})
  }
//]]>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/134770._gota_Krist_f">About Ágota Kristóf</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/134770._gota_Krist_f"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1389532281p3/134770.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/134770._gota_Krist_f">Ágota Kristóf
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5301372577673222016">Ágota Kristóf was a Hungarian writer, who lived in Switzerland and wrote in French. Kristof received the European prize for French literature for The Notebook (1986). She won the 2001 Gottfried Keller Award in Switzerland and the Austrian State Prize for European Literature in 2008. <br/><br/>Kristof's first steps as a writer were in the realm of poetry and theater (John et Joe, Un rat qui passe), which is </span>
  <span id="freeText5301372577673222016" style="display:none">Ágota Kristóf was a Hungarian writer, who lived in Switzerland and wrote in French. Kristof received the European prize for French literature for The Notebook (1986). She won the 2001 Gottfried Keller Award in Switzerland and the Austrian State Prize for European Literature in 2008. <br /><br />Kristof's first steps as a writer were in the realm of poetry and theater (John et Joe, Un rat qui passe), which is a facet of her works that did not have as great an impact as her trilogy. In 1986 Kristof’s first novel, The Notebook appeared. It was the beginning of a moving trilogy. The sequel titled The Proof came 2 years later. The third part was published in 1991 under the title The Third Lie. The most important themes of this trilogy are war and destruction, love and loneliness, promiscuous, desperate, and attention-seeking sexual encounters, desire and loss, truth and fiction.<br /><br />She has received the European prize for French literature for The Notebook. This novel was translated in more than 30 languages. In 1995 she published a new novel, Yesterday. Kristof also wrote a book called L'analphabète (in English The Illiterate) and published in 2004. This is an autobiographical text. It explores her love of reading as a young child, and we travel with her to boarding school, and over the border to Austria, and then to Switzerland. Forced to leave her country due to the failure of the anti-communist rebellion, she hopes for a better life in Zurich.<br /><br />The majority of her works were published by Editions du Seuil in Paris. She has two new short stories published at Mini Zoe collection entitled "Ou es-tu Mathias" and "Line, le temps". The names Mathias and Line are from her previous novels.<br /><br />She died on 27 July 2011 in her Neuchâtel home.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5301372577673222016" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_230521'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/230521.La_Preuve?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">La Preuve<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/134770._gota_Krist_f?origin=series_works\">Ágota Kristóf<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.02 avg rating &mdash; 3,011 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1988\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer11625982497575999666\">Avec Le Grand Cahier nous étions dans un pays en guerre où deux enfants, des jumeaux, apprenaient à survivre en usant toutes les ressources du mal et de la cruauté. Puis les jumeaux se séparaient, l’un d’eux franchissant la frontière, laissant l’autr<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11625982497575999666\" style=\"display:none\">Avec Le Grand Cahier nous étions dans un pays en guerre où deux enfants, des jumeaux, apprenaient à survivre en usant toutes les ressources du mal et de la cruauté. Puis les jumeaux se séparaient, l’un d’eux franchissant la frontière, laissant l’autre en son pays pacifié mais dominé par son régime autoritaire. Seul, désormais privé d’une partie de lui-même, Lucas, celui resté, semble vouloir se consacrer au bien. Il recueille Yasmine et adopte son fils Mathias, porte sa pitance au curé du village, tente de consoler Clara dont le mari fut pendu pour ‘‘trahison’’, écoute avec attention la confession de Victor, le libraire qui rêve d’écrire un livre ... Et si c’était pire ? Le propre d’un système totalitaire n’est-il pas de pervertir à la base tout élan de générosité ? Ce que découvrira Claus, le jumeau exilé de retour sur les lieux de ses premiers forfaits, sera plus terrible encore : qu’il n’y a pas de générosité sans crime, et qu’on est toujours deux, même quand on est seul.\n\nAu-delà de la fable, l’auteur pousuit ici son exploration impitoyable d’une mémoire si longtemps divisée, à l’image de l’Europe, et nous livre une belle méditation désespérée sur la littérature.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11625982497575999666\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer       <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2075023-le-grand-cahier---la-preuve---le-troisi-me-mensonge">Quotes from The Notebook, The...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I answer that I try to write true stories but that at a given point the story becomes unbearable because of it’s very truth, and then I have to change it. I tell her that I try to tell my story but all of a sudden I can’t-I don’t have the courage, it hurts too much. And so I embellish everything and describe things not as they happened but the way I wished they happened.<br />She says, “Yes, there are lives sadder than the saddest of books.” I say, “Yes. No book, no matter how sad, can be as sad as a life.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
